Alexandre Ghiti

1 items
Patches Submitted (1)
Last comment 2026-07-18 [PATCH 0/2] mm: zswap: free cold writeback folios promptly 2 patches
Show 2 individual patches
  • [1/2] mm: zswap: free synchronous-IO writeback folios directly Neutral Last comment 2026-07-18 Kairui Song
  • [2/2] mm: zswap: deferred dropbehind free of writeback folios
Neutral LLM

This patch series aims to improve the performance of zswap by freeing cold writeback folios promptly after writeback completion, reducing unnecessary reclaim scans and keeping cold memory resident for shorter periods.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Kairui Song ↳ replying to Alexandre Ghiti Neutral LLM
Suggested using a bool skip_lru instead of freeing the folio directly, and proposed renaming swap_cache_alloc_folio to __swap_cache_alloc_folio with updated kdoc.
Show original comment 
On Sat, Jul 18, 2026 at 11:36:39AM +0800, Alexandre Ghiti wrote:
> When zswap writes an entry back, it allocates a swap cache folio,
> decompresses the entry into it and writes it out. That folio is cold by
> construction, but it is currently left on the LRU for page reclaim to find
> and free later. This wastes a reclaim scan and keeps cold memory resident
> longer than necessary.
> 
> For synchronous-IO swap devices writeback completes in the calling context,
> so the folio can be freed right after the write rather than left behind; do
> that. Because it is freed directly rather than through reclaim, it is
> allocated off the LRU: dropping the last reference on a folio still on the
> LRU would trip the free-time page-flag checks. A folio that a concurrent
> swapin has meanwhile claimed is left in place and reclaimed as usual.
> 
> Asynchronous and filesystem-backed swap complete writeback in interrupt
> context, where the folio cannot be freed; they are handled in a later
> change.
> 
> Signed-off-by: Alexandre Ghiti <alex@ghiti.fr>
> ---
>  mm/swap.h       |  3 ++-
>  mm/swap_state.c | 17 +++++++++++------
>  mm/zswap.c      | 36 ++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++--
>  3 files changed, 47 insertions(+), 9 deletions(-)
>

>  			    struct folio *folio, swp_entry_t entry);

Compared to the bool skip_lru here, will this work better? (not tested)

Boris Burkov

7 items
Patches Submitted (1)
Last comment 2026-07-15 [PATCH v2] btrfs: write-protect folios during data writeback Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds write-protection for folios during data writeback in the Btrfs file system to prevent corruption and data loss due to concurrent writes by processes with an mmap-ed file. The patch addresses issues related to large folios, subpage sectorsize, zoned submissions, inline extents, and EOF-spanning folios, where a process can modify a sector while it is being used by writeback without faulting, leading to corruption or lost writes.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Qu Wenruo ↳ replying to Boris Burkov Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the patch does not handle the case where a task with an existing mmap-ed PTE writes to a folio before it is fully submitted and the dirty bit is cleared, which can result in losing the write even if fsync() is called.
Show original comment 
Reviewed-by: Qu Wenruo <wqu@suse.com>

Thanks,
Qu

Re: [PATCH v2] btrfs: add verity Merkle folio to page cache after reading it Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-07-18 Re: [PATCH 3/4] btrfs: handle ENOMEM from btrfs_insert_dir_item() without aborting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a concern about handling ENOMEM errors in btrfs_insert_dir_item() without causing an abort, specifically focusing on whether this approach will lead to further ENOMEM errors in other functions like btrfs_orphan_add(). The patch aims to provide more concrete evidence that this handling strategy is effective and worth the added complexity.

5 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Jeff Layton ↳ replying to Boris Burkov Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Jeff Layton expressed concerns about the practicality of reproducing the ENOMEM issue in btrfs_insert_dir_item() under heavy memory pressure, but noted that there are records of filesystem aborts where this patch would have prevented them. He suggested using ALLOW_ERROR_INJECTION() to make testing easier.
Show original comment 
I didn't really try to reproduce this in earnest. We only see it in our
fleet under heavy memory pressure, and even then at such low frequency,
I doubt our chances of hitting this on anything other than a huge set
of machines.

So, theoretical / structural, but we have record of filesystem aborts
where the stack indicates that this would have prevented it. Userland
would have gotten an -ENOMEM back but the fs wouldn't have aborted.

I see that there are some ALLOW_ERROR_INJECTION() calls in btrfs. We
could wire some of these functions up with that, which would make this
easier to test. I'll look into that in the meantime.
View on lore ↗
Signals: concern about practicality, request for additional testing
Boris Burkov (author) ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Needs Work LLM
Author acknowledges that handling ENOMEM in btrfs_add_link() does not necessarily prevent aborts in btrfs_orphan_add(), and requests more concrete evidence of the patch's benefits.
Show original comment 
My concern is not that we don't hit ENOMEM in btrfs_add_link(), since
like you said we can observe that in abort logs. I am worried that even
if we try to handle it gracefully, we will just ENOMEM in
btrfs_orphan_add() and abort anyway. That is why I was wanting to see
some more concrete evidence this actually helps to make it worth the
complexity.
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledges a limitation, requests further evidence
Jeff Layton ↳ replying to Boris Burkov Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Jeff Layton expressed concern that even if ENOMEM is handled gracefully, the patch may still result in an abort due to subsequent errors in btrfs_orphan_add(), and suggested preallocating the main allocation in that codepath by stashing it in a new pointer in btrfs_trans_handle().
Show original comment 
In the case where we handle this gracefully, we won't hit that because
it will have returned -ENOMEM before that point. But, you do have a
good point that we could allocate these objects successfully, and then
hit an error in btrfs_orphan_add() anyway.

One thought: It looks like the main allocation in that codepath is
btrfs_alloc_path()? We could consider preallocating that too -- maybe
stash it in a new pointer in btrfs_trans_handle?

Thanks! This is good food for thought.

Author responded to Jeff Layton's concern about the patch handling ENOMEM in btrfs_orphan_add() by explaining that when ENOMEM occurs, it will instead call btrfs_orphan_add(), which is likely to also fail with ENOMEM. Author expressed confusion about reviewer's concern, stating they had provided inline comments near the relevant code and apologizing for any misunderstanding. Author acknowledges reviewer's concern that handling ENOMEM in btrfs_insert_dir_item() might not be sufficient, as it could still lead to an abort in btrfs_orphan_add(). Author promises to resend evidence to support the complexity of the patch.

Qu Wenruo ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Qu Wenruo suggested using on-stack memory for btrfs_alloc_path() to avoid ENOMEM issues, citing the practice used in btrfs-progs and the small size of the structure (112 bytes), but did not recommend making this change universally for all btrfs_path instances.
Show original comment 
For btrfs_alloc_path(), you can just use on-stack memory for that.

That's the practice we utilize for btrfs-progs, and it's still 
acceptable for kernel, since that structure is only 112 bytes.

We should not switch all btrfs_path to on-stack ones, but for critical 
ones that memory allocation can lead to trans abort, I'd say it's 
definitely worth the extra on-stack memory usage.

Thanks,
Qu

Last comment 2026-07-18 Re: [PATCH 3/4] btrfs: handle ENOMEM from btrfs_insert_dir_item() without aborting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the handling of ENOMEM errors in the btrfs_insert_dir_item() function, preventing it from abruptly aborting when an out-of-memory condition occurs. Instead, it calls btrfs_orphan_add(), which is also likely to fail due to memory constraints. The change aims to provide a more controlled and recoverable behavior in such situations.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Jeff Layton ↳ replying to Boris Burkov Neutral LLM
Reviewer suggested combining Boris's approach with Qu's suggestion to fix the issue, and offered to implement it in v2.
Show original comment 
Interesting. Between that approach and Qu's suggestion we should be
able fix this up. I'll look at doing that for v2.

Thanks again for the review!

-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

Gregory Price

2 items
Patches Submitted (1)
Ongoing Submitted 2026-07-01 Last comment 2026-07-13 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH 0/2] mm: don't apply task mempolicy to unmovable kernel allocations 2 patches
Show 2 individual patches
  • [1/2] mm/filemap: place page-cache folios via an explicit mempolicy Submitted 2026-07-01
  • [2/2] mm/mempolicy: skip task mempolicy for unmovable unaccounted kernel allocations Submitted 2026-07-01
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series prevents a task's NUMA memory policy from influencing incidental kernel allocations that have no direct relationship to the task. Currently, these allocations follow the task's policy, which can lead to poor placement on tiered-memory systems and cause regressions. The fix is to only apply the task policy to movable allocations explicitly tied to the task via __GFP_ACCOUNT, while preferring node-local placement for unmovable kernel allocations. This change ensures that kernel memory is placed in a way that minimizes performance regressions and maintains existing behavior for page cache placement.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Huang, Ying Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Huang, Ying expressed theoretical agreement but requested empirical evidence of the patch's benefits. Reviewer noted that the patch does not handle the case where a task's mempolicy is set to MPOL_F_LOCAL, which can lead to incorrect placement of unmovable kernel allocations.
Show original comment 
Personally, I think this should be the right thing to do theoretically.
However, you may need to find some practical issues that this resolves.

---

---
Best Regards,
Huang, Ying
View on lore ↗
Signals: requested evidence, patch needs additional handling for MPOL_F_LOCAL policy
Gregory Price (author) ↳ replying to Huang, Ying Needs Work LLM
The author acknowledges that there is at least one scenario where the patch improves performance, specifically when BIOS-configured CXL memory is brought up in ZONE_NORMAL and a task interleave policy causes unaccounted kernel allocations to land on the remote node. The author agrees that this is an improvement but notes it's not a complete fix and plans to send a v2 with additional fixes.
Show original comment 
I don't entirely disagree, but there is at least one scenario where this
is an obvious improvement:

  BIOS-configured CXL memory brought up in ZONE_NORMAL. 

An task interleave policy on such a system will end up with unaccounted
kernel allocations landing on the remote node, which is just not
preferable at all (and uncorrectable).

It's not a complete fix (fallbacks can still occur under pressure), but
it's one piece of the puzzle.


Forward looking:  This patch makes private-node's with ZONE_NORMAL
reliably hot-un-pluggable.  But that improvement is obviously predicated
on work that isn't upstream (yet :] ).

I need to send a v2 of this with SLAB_ACCOUNT fixed up, and some numbers
to justify dropping the pagecache fix.  But I will probably sandbag this
a bit until i finally send out v5 of private nodes.

~Gregory

Signals: acknowledges improvement, plans to revise

Last comment 2026-07-18 [PATCH 0/4] btrfs: handle -ENOMEM errors in some synchronous dirops without aborting 4 patches
Show 4 individual patches
  • [1/4] btrfs: split btrfs_insert_delayed_dir_index() into prealloc and commit phases
  • [2/4] btrfs: pre-allocate delayed dir index before btree modification

  • [4/4] btrfs: pre-allocate delayed dir index for non-overwrite rename
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a concern about handling ENOMEM errors in btrfs_insert_dir_item() without causing an abort, specifically focusing on whether this approach will lead to further ENOMEM errors in other functions like btrfs_orphan_add(). The patch aims to provide more concrete evidence that this handling strategy is effective and worth the added complexity.

6 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Boris Burkov Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Boris Burkov questioned whether the patch addresses a real-world issue, citing concerns that allocations involved in btrfs_orphan_add() might succeed while those for delayed_node fail due to their similar size and potential memory requirements under reclaim conditions.
Show original comment 
I feel like the crux of this series to me is whether you have practical
conditions where the allocation of the delayed_node is failing, but the
allocations involved in btrfs_orphan_add() succeed. It allocates a
btrfs_path and has to walk the btree which might have to read the node
at every level which might need to allocate 16k extent buffers and
extent buffer objects and xarray storage for each one. For size
reference, on my build (maybe debug..?) a delayed_node is 552 bytes,
while a btrfs_path is 112 and an extent_buffer is 432. So they are
pretty similar in size (not to mention the 16k of node file backed
memory we are sort of likely to have to allocate if we are under
reclaim)

Were you able to reproduce this issue and help in practice or is this a
theoretical / structural improvement?

With that said, all the prealloc wiring looks good to me in general, and
it seems to be a pretty clean win for the "name exists" case in the next
patch.

Thanks,
Boris

Neutral LLM

This patch series converts the nfsstat server-side interfaces to use netlink, addressing limitations in the current procfs interface such as global counters and fragile parsing format. It adds per-network-namespace procedure call counts to the sunrpc layer and exposes them through a new netlink handler in the nfsd generic netlink family.

Last comment 2026-07-16 Re: [PATCH v6 3/6] nfsd: implement server-stats-get netlink handler Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch removes the collection of a separate stat for wdeleg_getattr, as it is not directly tied to the NFS protocol like other stats. The only difference between wdeleg_getattr and CB_GETATTR is how they handle concurrent GETATTR requests. The patch seeks to simplify the code by removing this redundant stat.

Author acknowledged Chuck's concern about the relevance of collecting wdeleg_getattr stat separately, and decided to add a use-case before deciding whether to count it like before.
Show original comment 
More or less. IIRC:

We had decided that wdeleg_getattr was sort of an oddity that didn't
really belong in this /proc file. The values in that file are mostly
counting other very specific protocol operations, but wdeleg_getattr is
basically counting GETATTR operations from other clients while there is
an outstanding write delegation on a file.

Since CB_GETATTR counts should approximate wdeleg_getattr, we decided
to start counting those instead (along with all of the other callback
ops). wdeleg_getattr in this series is changed to just report
CB_GETATTR counts.

---

Sure. We could add some way to keep reporting it (or just keep using
/proc for this). AFAIK though, there is no (public) consumer of this
stat. nfs-utils doesn't look at it.

To wit: I'd like to head a clear use-case for this stat before we
decide to we need to count it like we did before.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-30 Last comment 2026-07-13 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v5 0/6] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy 6 patches
Show 6 individual patches
  • [1/6] fuse: decouple fuse_ring creation from ent registration Submitted 2026-06-30
  • [2/6] fuse: add FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_ADD_QUEUE Submitted 2026-06-30
  • [3/6] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools Submitted 2026-06-30
  • [4/6] fuse: support registered buffer pools in io-uring Submitted 2026-06-30
  • [5/6] fuse: add zero-copy over io-uring Submitted 2026-06-30
  • [6/6] docs: fuse: document io-uring buffer pool and zero-copy uapi Neutral Submitted 2026-06-30Last comment 2026-07-02 Amir Goldstein Joanne Koong (author)
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds support for io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy to the FUSE file system. The current implementation tightly couples entries and buffers, requiring a dedicated payload buffer for each entry. This patch decouples them by allowing servers to pass in a buffer pool that the kernel can use as needed. Additionally, it introduces zero-copy functionality, which allows servers to directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through an intermediary buffer. The benefits of this patch include reduced memory usage requirements and improved throughput, particularly when backing I/O is not the bottleneck.

Signals: acknowledged mistake, need for reevaluation, acknowledged need for additional explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed concerns about potential buffer leaks, head-of-line blocking, and resource management in the zero-copy patch. They explained that there is no buffer leak as the buffers are allocated by userspace and released when the ring is torn down. They also stated that the decoupling of ents and buffers under buffer pools does not introduce additional head-of-line blocking and that buffer recycling does not need to check for stalled requests or idle entries. The author agreed to add a barrier pairing in v6 for robustness and dropped the WARN_ON_ONCE warning.
Show original comment 
Sashiko had some comments [1]. These are the replies to Sashiko's comments:

Patch 1/6:
1) Will add missing smp_rmb() in v6. Fix for pre-existing missing
smp_rmb() is submitted in [2].

2) Pre-existing issue, fix is submitted in [3].

Patch 2/6:
1) Pre-existing issue, fix is submitted in [3].

Patch 3/6:
1) ->uring_cmd() cancellation happens when a task exits or when a ring
is torn down. In both cases, the buffer is not marked as available
again in the bitmap, but this doesn't matter since the task/ring is
torn down. There's no buffer leak anywhere - the buffer was allocated
by userspace.

2) This introduces no additional head-of-line blocking than what
currently exists when there's no ent/buffer available for a request.
If a server wants to preserve memory and allocate fewer buffers than
entries, then requests have to wait until there are available buffers.
With ents and buffers decoupled under buffer pools, this also opens an
optimization where non-payload requests which require no buffer can
still be serviced when entries are free, but that can be a future
patch.
Buffer recycling does not need to check for stalled requests or idle
entries. The buffer is returned back when an in-flight request
completes, and each completion (commit-and-fetch) automatically
assigns the free buffer to any request waiting to be serviced.

3) It is safe (and expected) to read directly from cmd->sqe. uring_cmd
has different SQE-stability semantics than non-cmd io-uring ops.

4) A payload carrying request is added to the tail of the queue and
does not jump ahead of other payload carrying requests. Only
payloadless requests would get serviced first in the case of
bufferpool exhaustion, which is safe since the requests that can be
reordered are independent.

Patch 4/6:
1) It is safe (and expected) to read directly from cmd->sqe. uring_cmd
has different SQE-stability semantics than non-cmd io-uring ops.

2) This race is not reachable on a correct server. For a
buggy/malicious server that fires COMMIT_AND_FETCH concurrently with
ADD_BUFPOOL, this is safe since this returns an error regardless.
There's no regression introduced, but the barrier pairing should be
added anyways for robustness. I'll add this in for v6.

3) No, this is not necessary. See answer to 1)

Patch 5/6:

1) I'll drop the WARN_ON_ONCE. I don't think loopback mount can
trigger the warning, but I think other cases that go through the
kernel_read()/kernel_write() path on a direct-io zero-copy-enabled
fuse file potentially could.

2) This compiles, it is just currently flagged as broken because it
requires the io-uring bvec changes queued up in Jens's tree being
available [4].

3) same answer as above for 2)

4) same answer as the answer for 2) in patch 4.

5) There is no permanent leak - all resources are released when the
ring is torn down. This scenario is only possible if a server
deliberately tries to manufacture it by crossing rings. The worst case
that happens here is that the index in the buffer table of the
original ring remains reserved still and the folios are pinned, until
the ring is torn down. There's no use after free, as io-uring's node
refcount protects against this. Given that zero-copy is already gated
only to privileged servers and all resources are cleaned up when the
ring is torn down, this is not worth fixing, as the server would have
to deliberately self-inflict this.

6) No, this analysis is wrong. io_uring's teardown logic keeps
retrying cancellation until the ctx's references are all dropped (see
io_ring_exit_work()). This is the same in the task exit path as well
(io_uring_cancel_generic()).

Patch 6/6:
1) Will fix the typo for v6.

2) This is fine. It is safe (and expected) to read directly from
cmd->sqe. uring_cmd has different SQE-stability semantics than non-cmd
io-uring ops.

Thanks,
Joanne

[PATCH v6 0/6] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy 6 patches
Show 6 individual patches
  • [1/6] fuse: decouple fuse_ring creation from ent registration
  • [2/6] fuse: add FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_ADD_QUEUE
  • [3/6] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools
  • [4/6] fuse: support registered buffer pools in io-uring
  • [5/6] fuse: add zero-copy over io-uring
  • [6/6] docs: fuse: document io-uring buffer pool and zero-copy uapi
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series adds support for io-uring buffer pools to the FUSE file system, allowing servers to pass in a contiguous chunk of memory that the kernel can use to service requests. This decouples the request and payload buffers, reducing memory usage requirements and enabling optimizations for assigning payload memory. Additionally, the series introduces zero-copy functionality, which allows servers with CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges to directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through an intermediary buffer. The zero-copy feature requires the use of registered bvec changes in io-uring and buffer pools. The patches have been benchmarked to show significant throughput improvements for direct-I/O operations, particularly when backing I/O is not a bottleneck.

The patch series addresses two issues in the fuse driver related to io-uring initialization, specifically missing barriers that can lead to data corruption or incorrect behavior. The patches introduce additional barriers to ensure correct synchronization and fix potential bugs.

On 7/15/26 19:43, Joanne Koong wrote:
> fuse_block_alloc() reads fch->initialized and then fch->io_uring.
> fch->io_uring is set before fch->initialized, ordered by the smp_wmb()
> in fuse_chan_set_intialized(), but fuse_block_alloc() has no matching
> read barrier between the two loads.
> 
> This may lead a CPU to observe fch->initialized=1 but fch->io_uring=0,
> and skip the check that blocks request allocation until the io-uring
> queues are ready. This can reintroduce the lock-order inversion deadlock
> that commit 3393ff964e0f prevents.
> 
> Add an smp_rmb() barrier to pair with the smp_wmb() in
> fuse_chan_set_initialized() to prevent this.
>

Bernd Schubert ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Positive LLM
Recommended using READ_ONCE/WRITE_ONCE in fuse_uring_destruct() for consistency and added another Reviewed-by tag.
Show original comment 

On 7/15/26 19:43, Joanne Koong wrote:
> fuse_uring_create_queue() initializes a fuse_ring_queue and then
> publishes the pointer into ring->queues[qid] with WRITE_ONCE() under the
> fch->lock. There are several readers that may concurrently be fetching
> that pointer locklessly and then deferencing it.
> 
> WRITE_ONCE() doesn't ensure ordering of the queue's field
> initialization before the ring->queues[qid] pointer assignment. The
> queue must be published with smp_store_release() so the field
> initialization is guaranteed to happen before.
> 
> Readers in paths where the read may happen concurrently with the store
> need to use READ_ONCE() because any race involving a plain access is
> undefined.
> 
> Fixes: 24fe962c86f5 ("fuse: {io-uring} Handle SQEs - register commands")
> Cc: stable@vger.kernel.org

Last comment 2026-07-17 Re: [PATCH v6 1/6] fuse: decouple fuse_ring creation from ent registration Neutral LLM

The patch aims to decouple fuse_ring creation from entity registration, but the discussion reveals a deeper issue of synchronization between userspace and kernel initialization.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Miklos Szeredi ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Needs Work LLM
Identified a potential synchronization issue between userspace and kernel initialization, suggesting that the REGISTER operation should block until initialization is complete.
Show original comment 
On Fri, 17 Jul 2026 at 18:28, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> On Fri, Jul 17, 2026 at 2:32 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
> >
> > On Thu, 16 Jul 2026 at 19:59, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
> > >
> > > Currently, the connection's fuse_ring is created lazily on the first
> > > FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER command. A server registers entries from one
> > > thread per queue (one per CPU) and those threads issue their first
> > > REGISTER command concurrently. They then race to create the single
> > > per-connection fuse_ring, which required open-coded handling in
> > > fuse_uring_create() to detect and protect against concurrent creations.
> > >
> > > Decouple fuse_ring creation from ent registration and move it to
> > > FUSE_INIT reply processing after a server has negotiated and set
> > > FUSE_OVER_IO_URING. The ring is published before the connection is
> > > marked initialized. fuse_uring_register() no longer creates the ring and
> > > it instead uses the ring set up at init time.
> >
> > What about if the fuse ring is not yet set up?  Should
> > fuse_uring_register() wait in that case?
>
> The fuse ring is set up when the kernel processes the FUSE_INIT reply.
> If userspace races ahead and issues a REGISTER before the FUSE_INIT
> reply is processed, they get back -EAGAIN and will need to retry,
> which is the same as the pre-existing behavior. The ring gets
> published before fch->initialized gets set.
>
> Not sure if this answers your question or if I'm misreading what you're asking.

That was my question, yes.

Retrying in a loop doesn't sound like good behavior.

I was thinking that with sync INIT the completing the write() with the
INIT reply does not mean that the connection gets initialized.

Shouldn't the REGISTER op block until initialization is complete?

Thanks,
Miklos

Re: [PATCH v6 1/6] fuse: decouple fuse_ring creation from ent registration Neutral LLM
Last comment 2026-07-15 Re: [PATCH] fuse: disable default bdi strictlimiting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch disables the default strictlimiting of block device I/O (bdi) for FUSE file systems, which can lead to inaccurate dirty page statistics and excessive writeback. The issue arises from periodic flushing of memory cgroup stats, causing lagging values that result in under-reporting of dirty pages. This can cause writers to exceed the limit by more than 2x before throttling kicks in. The patch aims to address this problem by disabling strictlimiting for FUSE file systems.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Jan Kara ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the balance_dirty_pages() code uses lagging memcg stats, leading to under-reported values and potential overrunning of the dirty limit, suggesting a possible solution to flush based on the number of pages getting dirtied instead of elapsed time. Reviewer noted that the recent change to use mem_cgroup_flush_stats_ratelimited() in mem_cgroup_wb_stats() causes applications to overshoot memcg dirty limits by several gigabytes, particularly for small memcgs, and suggested reverting this change or taking into account the size of memcg when ratelimiting.
Show original comment 
I see. That would indeed explain why we can overrun the dirty limit so
much.

---

Checking some git history, this actually seems to be a relatively recent
change from Shakeel (added to CC) - d9b3ce8769e3 ("mm: writeback: ratelimit
stat flush from mem_cgroup_wb_stats"). So you can check whether reverting
that change makes dirty throttling not overshoot so much.

Shakeel, your change to use mem_cgroup_flush_stats_ratelimited() in
mem_cgroup_wb_stats() results in applications overshooting memcg dirty
limits by several gigabytes which for small memcgs (with 8GB memory in this
case, which means a dirty limit of ~1.5GB) is a considerable issue. I guess
the ratelimiting should take into account the size of memcg (or ideally the
dirty limit in the case of mem_cgroup_wb_stats()) as well. Using
somewhat outdated numbers for dirty throttling is fine but when we can
overshoot the dirty limit more than three times, it leads to rather long
stalls in dirty throttling and similar issues...

								Honza

Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Jan Kara Needs Work LLM
Author addressed Jan Kara's concern about high dirty page counts exceeding limits by reverting a specific commit (d9b3ce8769e3) which fixes the issue, resulting in bounded dirty pages and reduced latency. Author acknowledges that reverting commit d9b3ce8769e3 might be the simplest fix due to reduced global lock contention, but is open to exploring alternative solutions if reverting does not address the issue.
Show original comment 
Reverting d9b3ce8769e3 fixes the issue. On xfs, I'm now seeing the
number of dirty pages stays bounded by the limit instead of going 2x+
over, and the max latency is now around ~0.33s instead of ~4s.

---

It looks like commit d9b3ce8769e3 was landed in Feb 2024 when rstat
was still using the single global lock. I see commit 748922dcfabd
("cgroup: use subsystem-specific rstat locks to avoid contention")
later in the tree, which was landed around May 2025 and reduces that
global lock contention. That might make the cost of the flush a lot
lower than what it was when the ratelimit was needed in d9b3ce8769e3,
so maybe reverting d9b3ce8769e3 wouldn't make much of a difference for
write-heavy workloads and would be the simplest fix? If it does still
regress, I'd be happy to look into making the flush based on how many
pages have been dirtied and scaled to the dirty limit, if that sounds
like the best way forward. I tried running the sysbench
oltp_write_only benchmark mentioned in d9b3ce8769e3 on my setup but
couldn't get the database to dirty pages fast enough to trigger the
balance dirty pages logic to see how it performs without the
ratelimiting. If you still have the script for how you ran the test
back in 2024, I'm happy to give it a go on my local machine.

Thanks,
Joanne

Signals: fix_applied, acknowledges a potential fix, open to further investigation
Last comment 2026-07-16 Re: [PATCH] fuse: disable default bdi strictlimiting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch reverts a previous change that disabled default bdi (block device interface) strictlimiting, which was intended to prevent the number of dirty pages from exceeding a certain limit. However, it's been found that this change may not be necessary anymore due to subsequent changes in the cgroup locking mechanism, and reverting it resolves performance issues on xfs file systems. The patch suggests that the flush rate limiting might no longer be needed with the current locking scheme, making this revert a simple fix.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Shakeel Butt ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that reverting d9b3ce8769e3 might not be the best solution as it was landed after a change to reduce global lock contention, which could make the flush cost lower and thus render the ratelimiting unnecessary. Reviewer noted that even with per-subsystem locks, flushing is still expensive due to kernel traversing the cgroup update tree for each CPU on the system, and suggested moving specific stats from rstat to a separate mechanism where updates can be more expensive but reads can be cheap. Reviewer expressed preference for a more sophisticated rate limiting approach over reverting the patch, suggesting that a general solution should be explored first
Show original comment 
Hi Jan & Joanne,

On Wed, Jul 15, 2026 at 06:28:16PM -0700, Joanne Koong wrote:

---

Unfortunetely global lock was just part of the cost and even per-subsystem
lock reduces contention across subsystems but flushing is still expensive as the
kernel may have to traverse the cgroup update tree for each cpu on the system.

CCing Yosry who is looking into similar issue but for zswap stats. Maybe a
general solution for such specific stats is to move them from rstat to a
separate mechanism where update can be a bit more expensive but the read side
can be cheap.

---

First let's see if the general solution is doable and simple otherwise we can
explore use-case specific solutions. In extreme case revert is an options as
well but I would prefer a more sophisticated ratelimiting (that Jan suggested)
than a revert.

Re: [PATCH v2] mm/vmscan: clear hopeless kswapd when global direct reclaim makes progress Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-07-15 Re: [PATCH v2] mm/vmscan: clear hopeless kswapd when global direct reclaim makes progress Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the hopeless kswapd mechanism in Linux's memory management system. The goal is to prevent kswapd from becoming overly aggressive when global direct reclaim makes progress, which can lead to an imbalance between watermark levels and actual memory availability. The patch addresses a specific bug where a single direct reclaimer getting lucky triggers a burst of kswapd activity, causing problems even if the unreclaimable set exceeds available memory.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Altan Hacigumus ↳ replying to Johannes Weiner Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the patched kswapd latches and unlatches in bursts of 16 passes, each triggered by a direct reclaim success, which is different from the baseline behavior where kswapd latches once and stays down. Reviewer Altan Hacigumus noted that the distinction between global direct reclaim and memcg reclaim is not meaningful in this case, as memory.low/memory.min makes a bad signal worse but does not make the global reclaim signal good. He also pointed out that the current patch may reintroduce a problem that was recently fixed by moving trigger points around. Reviewer Altan Hacigumus expressed concern that the patch does not address the root cause of the issue, as kswapd still runs indefinitely under sustained pressure, and suggested reviewing the history of the hopeless kswapd concept to avoid reintroducing a problem Reviewer Altan Hacigumus pointed out that the distinction between memory.low/memory.min and global reclaim is not meaningful in this case, as it only makes a bad signal worse without making the global reclaim signal good.
Show original comment 
It does, and I see it in the runs: the patched kswapd latches and
unlatches in 16-pass bursts, one per direct reclaim success, where the
baseline latches once and stays down. Each burst is bounded - the
revived kswapd hits MAX_RECLAIM_RETRIES zero-reclaim runs and parks
again. Keeping it up takes a new success every time - a task in the
slowpath, and something left to reclaim.

---

No progress event (global or memcg) says kswapd can balance the node.
The difference is what sets them off. memory.high re-triggers on the
cgroup's own usage (whatever the node is doing) - so a cgroup parked
at its limit keeps kswapd cycling even when the node needs nothing.
OTOH global direct reclaim only runs when the page allocator itself is
below the watermarks. The workload behind those numbers has no memcg
reclaim in it at all - the gate went in for the memory.high, and it is
parking kswapd here.

---

Under sustained pressure this node does not balance either - thats the
workload this came from. kswapd keeps running there for as long as the
node stays starved, and that is what the patch is for. The reclaim has
to happen either way, the only question is whether every allocating task
does it synchronously or kswapd does it in the background. That is the
413441 -> 6532 in the changelog, with pgsteal moving from direct to
kswapd. When the demand stops, the resets stop with it and kswapd parks.

---

The changelog cites dc9fe9b7056a and the any-progress behaviour before
it. c73322d098e4, which introduced the counter, describes kswapd
sleeping "until a direct reclaimer manages to reclaim some pages, thus
proving the node reclaimable again".

That leaves the tradeoff: either the balanced-only reset stays and the
observed allocation stalls remain, or global direct reclaim may revive
kswapd and that work moves back to the background.

On 15/07/2026 14:54, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> On Tue, Jul 14, 2026 at 07:20:57AM -0700, Usama Arif wrote:
>> psi_group_change() walks the @clear and @set bitmasks to
>> decrement/increment groupc->tasks[t]. Both masks are at most
>> NR_PSI_TASK_COUNTS (=4) wide, dense at [0, 4), and typically
>> sparse. Today's form visits every position up to the highest set
>> bit:
>>
>> 	for (t = 0, m = clear; m; m &= ~(1 << t), t++) {
>> 		if (!(m & (1 << t)))
>> 			continue;
>> 		...
>> 	}
>>
>> so a mask with only bit 3 set still spins four times; the same
>> open-coded shape repeats for @set. The code is also unnecessarily
>> hard to read.
>>
>> Switch both walks to for_each_set_bit() which is easier to read
>> and also more efficient. As NR_PSI_TASK_COUNTS is a compile-time
>> constant <= BITS_PER_LONG, find_next_bit() folds into its
>> small_const_nbits() fast path (single load + GENMASK + __ffs), lowering
>> to a bit-scan where one exists (x86 TZCNT/BSF, arm64 RBIT+CLZ).
>>
>> psi_group_change() runs from psi_task_switch() and psi_task_change()
>> once per ancestor psi_group per event, so the saved iterations
>> multiply out on any hot scheduler workload.
>>
>> No functional change intended.
> 
> This actually started out using ffs. Because the performance is so
> sensitive in this path, this was handtuned to scheduler benchmarks.
> 
> https://lore.kernel.org/all/20180718120318.GC2476@hirez.programming.kicks-ass.net/
> 
> Not the worst idea to revisit this, but you have to be careful, look
> at the asm, and benchmark it. gcc is producing more code for me with
> your patch.

I built the below patch and the code size reduces as well by
67 bytes from 756B to 689B. If it looks ok, I can send it as v2?


>From 052c845707f65d58d5cf8cb1da6515e268159e83 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>
Date: Sat, 11 Jul 2026 03:57:01 -0700
Subject: [PATCH] sched/psi: use __ffs() to walk task-count bitmasks in
 psi_group_change()

psi_group_change() walks the @clear and @set bitmasks to
decrement/increment groupc->tasks[t]. Both masks are at most
NR_PSI_TASK_COUNTS (=4) wide and typically have one or two bits
set. Today's form visits every position up to the highest set bit:

	for (t = 0, m = clear; m; m &= ~(1 << t), t++) {
		if (!(m & (1 << t)))
			continue;
		...
	}

so a mask with only bit 3 set still spins four times through the
skip path. Switch both walks to __ffs() + m &= m-1 form:

	while (clear) {
		t = __ffs(clear);
		clear &= clear - 1;
		...
	}

which iterates only over the set bits and terminates naturally on
m == 0. m & (m - 1) clears the lowest set bit. This code is easier
to read as well.

An in-kernel microbench (noinline, same body, IRQs off, pinned CPU
on Zen4c/Bergamo, min-of-10 cyc/call) over mask distributions
produced by common scheduler PSI paths:

  mask pattern                        old    new    delta
  empty          (clear=0x0, set=0x0)  3.68   3.68    +0%
  sleep          (clear=0x4, set=0x0)  9.60   3.74   -61%
  iowait-sleep   (clear=0x4, set=0x1) 12.32   4.45   -63%
  memstall-sleep (clear=0xc, set=0x0) 11.87   5.54   -53%
  wake           (clear=0x0, set=0x4)  7.10   3.70   -47%
  iowait-wake    (clear=0x1, set=0x4) 10.83   4.48   -58%

Every non-empty case wins 47-63%: old cost tracks the highest set bit
(linear walk), new cost tracks the count of set bits (skip zeros via
TZCNT). Single-bit patterns run at the empty-case floor.

The generated psi_group_change() text also shrinks by 67 bytes under
-O2 -march=x86-64 (756 -> 689): no scratch register for a "constant 1"
(only __ffs's operand is needed), simpler bit-clear (LEA+AND vs
SHL+NOT+AND after the test), and no skip-if-unset check per position.

No functional change intended.

Signed-off-by: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>

On Mon, Jul 13, 2026 at 3:56 AM Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org> wrote:

> On Sun, Jul 12, 2026 at 07:46:19AM -1000, Tejun Heo wrote:
> > psi_schedule_rtpoll_work() is called locklessly from the scheduler hotpath
> > and can race psi_trigger_destroy() taking down the last rtpoll trigger under
> > rtpoll_trigger_lock:
> >
> >   psi_schedule_rtpoll_work()        psi_trigger_destroy()
> >
> >   rcu_read_lock();
> >   task = rcu_dereference(rtpoll_task);
> >                                     rcu_assign_pointer(rtpoll_task, NULL);
> >                                     timer_delete(&rtpoll_timer);
> >   mod_timer(&rtpoll_timer, ...);
> >   rcu_read_unlock();
> >                                     synchronize_rcu();
> >                                     kthread_stop(task_to_destroy);
> >
> > The group can then be freed with the re-armed timer still pending, and
> > poll_timer_fn() runs on freed memory.
> >
> > 461daba06bdc ("psi: eliminate kthread_worker from psi trigger scheduling
> > mechanism") deleted the timer synchronously after the synchronize_rcu(),
> > which prevented this but raced trigger creation instead: the deletion could
> > cancel the timer that a new trigger set armed during the grace period and,
> > as creation also reinitialized the timer at the time, corrupt it.
> > 8f91efd870ea ("psi: Fix race between psi_trigger_create/destroy") moved the
> > initialization into group_init() and the deletion into the locked section,
> > trading the creation races for the window above.
> >
> > Neither placement in the destruction path works. A pending timer firing
> > while the group is alive is harmless though. poll_timer_fn() just wakes the
> > rtpoll waitqueue and doesn't re-arm itself. Bind the timer to the group's
> > lifetime instead and shut it down in psi_cgroup_free(). Nothing can arm it
> > by then. timer_shutdown_sync() because the timer is never armed again.
> >

Last comment 2026-07-14 Re: [PATCH] mm/hugetlb: restore failed global reservations to subpool in alloc_hugetlb_folio Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch aims to restore failed global reservations to a subpool in the alloc_hugetlb_folio function, addressing an issue where hugetlb folios are not being properly allocated due to incorrect handling of subpools. The problem arises from competing fixes being proposed for this codepath, and this patch seeks to resolve it by restoring failed reservations to their original subpool. The approach taken involves revisiting the logic surrounding subpool locking and release to ensure correct behavior.

Song Hu ↳ replying to Joshua Hahn Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Song Hu noted that the patch's codepath is problematic due to multiple competing fixes being discussed, and specifically questioned why the subpool is unlocked or released in this particular code location. Reviewer Song Hu noted that unlock_or_release_subpool() should not be used in this error path because it may drop the subpool's last reference, which is not necessary since spool->count is never touched; instead, a plain spin_unlock_irqrestore() is sufficient and clearer.
Show original comment 
Hi Joshua,

Thanks for the review and for bringing the authors together.

First, apologies for the duplicate submission \u2014 when I sent this I was not
aware of the other proposals on the list ([1][2][3]). I ran into this leak
while looking at the subpool accounting and simply wanted to share a fix;
I did not mean to add noise to an ongoing discussion.

---

Good catch \u2014 it does not belong here. unlock_or_release_subpool() is meant
for paths that may drop the subpool's last reference (e.g.
hugepage_subpool_put_pages(), after which the subpool can become free). In
this error path we only adjust used_hpages and never touch spool->count, so
the subpool cannot be released here; a plain
spin_unlock_irqrestore(&spool->lock, flags) is sufficient and clearer. I
copied it from the hugetlb_reserve_pages() sibling fix (1d3f9bb4c8af), but
it is not the right helper here either; I will switch to
spin_unlock_irqrestore() in v2.

One thing that may be worth keeping in mind for the discussion:
a833a693a490 is already in 6.x -stable, so the leak is present in those
trees as well, and a backportable fix will be needed there regardless of
which direction is chosen for mainline.

For what it is worth, I verified the behavior with a small reproducer (a
size= hugetlbfs mount with nr_hugepages churned 10 -> 2 -> 10: unpatched,
the subpool only maps 2/10 pages after the leak; patched, 10/10), in case
it is useful to whoever ends up shaping the final fix.

Happy to help with whatever direction people want to take.

Thanks,
Song

[PATCH v4] fs/proc/task_mmu: fix PAGEMAP_SCAN written state for PMD holes Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in the Linux kernel where PAGEMAP_SCAN incorrectly reports an unpopulated PMD hole as not written, leading to data corruption after live migration or checkpoint/restore operations. The fix involves reporting holes in non-hugetlb uffd-wp VMAs as written and adding a selftest covering the anon-THP PMD-hole case. This patch addresses issues with write tracking and page table management, ensuring that changes made by MADV_DONTNEED are correctly reported to prevent data corruption.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-07-06 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH v3] iommu/arm-smmu-v3: Shrink command/event/PRI queues in kdump kernel Neutral LLM

This patch shrinks the command, event, and PRI queues in the kdump kernel to a single page when is_kdump_kernel() is true. The current implementation allocates these queues at their maximum size advertised by the hardware, which can be several megabytes each. This change reduces memory usage in the capture kernel, which runs from a small crashkernel reservation.

[PATCH v3] mm/huge_memory: unlock i_mmap_rwsem before releasing after-split folios Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug where the i_mmap_rwsem lock is not properly released before freeing after-split folios in the huge_memory module. The issue arises when splitting a THP (Transparent Huge Page) that reaches past the inode's size during truncation, causing a concurrent iput() to evict and RCU-free the inode before the i_mmap_unlock_read() function can touch it. To fix this, the patch moves the i_mmap_unlock_read() call to before the after-split unlock loop, ensuring that all mapping dereferences occur while the folio still pins the inode.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-07-14 Last comment 2026-07-16 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v2 0/5] mm: fix inode UAF when splitting a file folio past EOF 5 patches
Show 5 individual patches
  • [1/5] mm/memory-failure: keep the folio, not the poisoned subpage, locked across split Neutral Submitted 2026-07-14Last comment 2026-07-16 David (Arm) Kiryl Shutsemau Kiryl Shutsemau (author) Zi Yan Baolin Wang
  • [2/5] mm/huge_memory: refuse to split a file folio when the anchor is beyond EOF Submitted 2026-07-14
  • [3/5] mm/huge_memory: remove unused split_huge_page_to_order() Submitted 2026-07-14
  • [4/5] mm/huge_memory: remove unused can_split_folio() Submitted 2026-07-14
  • [5/5] mm/huge_memory: fold split_folio_to_list_to_order() into split_folio_to_order() Submitted 2026-07-14
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch set fixes a use-after-free (UAF) bug in the Linux kernel's inode handling when splitting a file folio past the end of file (EOF). The bug occurs because the inode is evicted from memory while still being accessed by the split function. To fix this, the patch changes the way memory_failure() splits files: instead of locking and splitting at the tail anchor, it now locks and splits at the head anchor, which remains in the page cache and keeps the inode pinned. Additionally, the patch makes it explicit that a file folio cannot be split when the anchor is beyond EOF, preventing future callers from reintroducing the bug. The changes are tagged for stable and have been compile- and boot-tested.

Kiryl Shutsemau (author) ↳ replying to David (Arm) Needs Work LLM
Author acknowledges that their original patch is broken due to incorrect pinning of the inode, and proposes a fix by introducing an -EBUSY guard in __folio_split() to refuse splits when the anchor is at or beyond EOF.
Show original comment 
Right. Non-uniform split can be useful.

But my patch is completely broken because code expects the pin to be on the
@page, not on the head. put_page() few lines down can explode already.

So the fix does not belong in memory_failure(). It belongs in
__folio_split(), and it is really just 2/5: refuse the split with -EBUSY
when @lock_at is at or beyond the sampled EOF.

The safety then sits in __folio_split() regardless of caller or split type,
which should also cover your non-uniform worry.

The behavioural change is that memory_failure() reports a beyond-EOF
poisoned tail as unsplit (MF_FAILED) instead of recovered, and kills the
mappers instead of splitting the page off. What we give up is salvaging
the folio's healthy pages and the clean unmap -- both low value for a page
that is beyond EOF and getting truncated away. Containment is unaffected:
PageHWPoison is set before the split and free_pages_prepare() keeps a
poisoned page out of the buddy allocator, so the bad page never comes back
regardless.

The alternative, if we would rather keep the recovered outcome, is to leave
@lock_at as the poisoned page and move i_mmap_unlock_read() (and
shmem_uncharge()) ahead of the after-split unlock loop, so every mapping
dereference happens while @folio -- the head, within EOF -- still pins the
inode. That is close to Hao's original patch. It works, but it rests on "no
mapping dereference after the unlock loop", which is its own fragility.

I lean towards the -EBUSY guard.

Comments?

-- 
  Kiryl Shutsemau / Kirill A. Shutemov
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledges a problem, proposes a new approach
Zi Yan ↳ replying to David (Arm) Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Zi Yan suggested an alternative approach to handle the inode locking issue, proposing to only use igrab() when @lock_at is at or beyond EOF. However, they noted that this approach has subtleties and may return -EBUSY unnecessarily for non-uniform splits, requiring a more complex calculation to determine the index of the after-split folio containing @lock_at. Reviewer Zi Yan noted that the patch introduces another implicit synchronization in the folio split process, which needs to be documented well. Reviewer Yan prefers moving i_mmap_unlock_read() before the unlock loop in __folio_split(), but is open to alternative solutions such as returning -EBUSY
Show original comment 
There is an alternative, only igrab() when @lock_at is at or beyond the EOF,
as I was bouncing ideas with Codex.

Some subtlety exists in these two “@lock_at at or beyond EOF” approaches.
When @lock_at can be a tail page of an after-split folio, Patch 2 returns
-EBUSY unnecessarily, since the after-split folio containing @lock_at will
be still in xarray, preventing inode going away. To make a precise decision,
we will need to determine the index of the after-split folio containing @lock_at
when checking against end. It is easy for uniform split, namely

lock_at_folio_index =
	folio->index + round_down(lock_at - &folio->page, 1UL << new_order);

but for non-uniform split, the calculation is more involved and I have not
figured it out yet.

If we go “return -EBUSY if @lock_at index is at or beyond EOF”, are we OK
with not being able to split a folio when it is actually splittable?

---

This approach works without changing existing behavior, as long as we document
the requirement well.

I admit that this is another implicit synchronization in folio split process,
in addition to
1) keeping original folio frozen until xarray is updated and
2) do not update xarray with after-split folios until after-split folios are
unfrozen.

---

I prefer moving i_mmap_unlock_read() before the unlock loop (shmem_uncharge()
is already before the loop), but I am more than happy to be convinced that
“return -EBUSY” is better or something else.

Best Regards,
Yan, Zi

Zi Yan ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Zi Yan suggested modifying patch 2 to avoid over-rejecting splits by checking if the lock_at_index is beyond EOF before returning -EBUSY, and also added a new function split_lock_at_index() to assist in calculating the lock_at_index for non-uniform splits.
Show original comment 
Your reasoning makes sense to me. Let's ignore this option.

For your patch 2, we might want something like below to avoid over
rejecting splits. WDYT?

offset = folio_page_idx(folio, lock_at);

if (split_type == SPLIT_TYPE_UNIFORM)
	lock_at_index = folio->index + round_down(offset, 1UL << new_order);
else
	/* @lock_at in non uniform split is always @folio */
	lock_at_index = folio->index;

if (lock_at_index >= end) {
	ret = -EBUSY;
	goto out_unlock;
}


Also to keep a record in case we want to have @lock_at pointing to any
tail page for non uniform split in the future, something like below is
going to be useful (assisted by Codex).

static pgoff_t split_lock_at_index(struct folio *folio,
		unsigned int new_order, struct page *split_at,
		struct page *lock_at, enum split_type split_type)
{
	unsigned long lock = folio_page_idx(folio, lock_at);
	unsigned long split = folio_page_idx(folio, split_at);
	int order;

	if (split_type == SPLIT_TYPE_UNIFORM)
		return folio->index + round_down(lock, 1UL << new_order);

	for (order = folio_order(folio) - 1; order >= new_order; order--) {
		unsigned long size = 1UL << order;
		unsigned long lock_base = round_down(lock, size);
		unsigned long split_base = round_down(split, size);

		/* folio containing @lock_at will not be split any more */
		if (lock_base != split_base)
			return folio->index + lock_base;
	}
	
	return folio->index + round_down(lock, 1UL << new_order);
}

if (split_lock_at_index(folio, new_order, split_at, lock_at, split_type) >= end) {
	ret = -EBUSY;
	goto out_unlock;
}

-- 
Best Regards,
Yan, Zi

* kernel BUG in __page_table_check_zero

Kiryl Shutsemau (author) ↳ replying to Zi Yan Needs Work LLM
Author addressed a concern about the order of unlocking and freeing after-split folios, agreeing that moving i_mmap_unlock_read() out of the window is more attractive to avoid complex -EBUSY condition. In v3, they restructured __folio_split() to drop i_mmap_rwsem right after remap_page(), before unlocking and freeing the after-split folios.
Show original comment 
Right. With the -EBUSY condition growing this hairy -- and having to stay
correct for non-uniform splits too -- just moving i_mmap_unlock_read() out
of the window looks more attractive.

This is really Hao's original patch with the reasoning corrected, so I kept
him as author. v3 below.

----------------------------------------------------------------------

From: "Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta)" <kas@kernel.org>
Subject: [PATCH v3] mm/huge_memory: unlock i_mmap_rwsem before releasing
 after-split folios

__folio_split() keeps dereferencing the mapping after the split:
shmem_uncharge(mapping->host) and remap_page() while the folios are still
frozen/locked, and i_mmap_unlock_read(mapping) at the very end, after the
after-split folios have been unlocked and freed.

Nothing holds an inode reference across that. The split relies on @folio
-- which the beyond-EOF drop loop never removes, as it starts at
folio_next(folio) -- staying locked and in the page cache to hold off
eviction. But the unlock loop unlocks @folio before i_mmap_unlock_read()
runs. If the caller's @lock_at is a tail beyond EOF, as memory_failure()
passes when splitting a poisoned tail of a shmem THP that reaches past
i_size during truncation, it too is gone from the page cache; so once
@folio is unlocked no locked, in-cache folio pins the inode, and a
concurrent final iput() can evict and RCU-free it before
i_mmap_unlock_read() touches i_mmap_rwsem:

  BUG: KASAN: slab-use-after-free in __up_read+0x634/0x790
   i_mmap_unlock_read include/linux/fs.h:537 [inline]
   __folio_split+0x732/0x1640 mm/huge_memory.c:4100
   try_to_split_thp_page+0xab/0x390 mm/memory-failure.c:1675
   memory_failure+0x1394/0x26e0 mm/memory-failure.c:2470

  Freed by task 4601:
   shmem_free_in_core_inode+0x54/0xb0 mm/shmem.c:5177
   evict+0x57f/0xac0 fs/inode.c:870

Do every mapping dereference while @folio still pins the inode: drop
i_mmap_rwsem right after remap_page(), before the loop that unlocks and
frees the after-split folios, and clear @mapping so the exit path does not
unlock it again. shmem_uncharge() and remap_page() already run before that
point, so after this nothing past the unlock loop touches the inode or the
mapping.

This is now a rule the split depends on, alongside keeping @folio frozen
until the page cache is updated: no inode or mapping dereference once the
after-split folios start being unlocked.

Reported-by: Hao Zhang <zhanghao1@kylinos.cn>
Closes: https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/20260710071344.GA106129@zh-pc
Fixes: baa355fd3314 ("thp: file pages support for split_huge_page()")
Cc: <stable@vger.kernel.org>
Co-developed-by: Hao Zhang <zhanghao1@kylinos.cn>
Signed-off-by: Hao Zhang <zhanghao1@kylinos.cn>
Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta) <kas@kernel.org>

diff --git a/mm/huge_memory.c b/mm/huge_memory.c

+++ b/mm/huge_memory.c
@@ -4109,6 +4109,18 @@ static int __folio_split(struct folio *folio, unsigned int new_order,

 	remap_page(folio, 1 << old_order, ttu_flags);

+	/*
+	 * Drop the mapping while the inode is still pinned. @folio stays
+	 * locked and present in the page cache until the loop below, so
+	 * eviction cannot free the inode yet; @lock_at is not enough, it may
+	 * be a tail beyond EOF that the split already dropped from the page
+	 * cache. Nothing past this point may touch the inode or the mapping.
+	 */
+	if (mapping) {
+		i_mmap_unlock_read(mapping);
+		mapping = NULL;
+	}
+
 	/*
 	 * Unlock all after-split folios except the one containing
 	 * @lock_at page. If @folio is not split, it will be kept locked.

View on lore ↗
Baolin Wang ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Reviewed-by Positive Heuristic
Gave Reviewed-by
View on lore ↗
Last comment 2026-07-14 [PATCH] mm: thp: pin the inode across a file folio split Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a use-after-free bug in the THP (Transparent Huge Pages) split function by pinning the inode across a file folio split using igrab(). The problem occurs when a concurrent final iput() can evict and RCU-free the inode before __folio_split() is done touching the mapping. The patch introduces a new variable 'inode' to hold the inode reference, which is grabbed with igrab() before the split and dropped with iput() after the last mapping dereference. If the inode is already being evicted, the function returns -EBUSY.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Zi Yan ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Reviewed-by Acked-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Zi Yan suggested moving the EOF folio drop code to avoid a shmem_uncharge() issue, but agreed that explicit inode pinning is a better solution due to an implicit dependency on inode lifetime. Gave Acked-by
Show original comment 
I was thinking maybe we could move the EOF folio drop code in the unlock
loop to avoid the shmem_uncharge() issue you mentioned in the Closes.
But that hides this implicit dependency (I did not know about this inode
lifetime issue until this patch comes out). So I agree that an explicit
inode pinning is a much better solution.

@lock_at is still in page cache we would self-deadlock.

This patch series fixes bugs in the handling of emulated port I/O operations in the TDX guest #VE handler. The first patch corrects an off-by-one error in the GENMASK() used for I/O size, while the third patch ensures that 32-bit port IN operations zero-extend into RAX as per x86 semantics. x86/tdx: Fix zero-extension for 32-bit port I/O

According to x86 architecture rules, 32-bit operations zero-extend the
result to 64 bits. The current implementation of handle_in() only masks
the lower 32 bits, which preserves the upper 32 bits of RAX when a
32-bit port IN instruction is emulated.

Use insn_assign_reg() to write the result back into RAX with proper
partial-register-write semantics: 1- and 2-byte forms leave the upper
bits untouched, the 4-byte form zero-extends to the full register.

Fixes: 03149948832a ("x86/tdx: Port I/O: Add runtime hypercalls")

CommitterDate: Mon, 13 Jul 2026 14:45:07 -07:00

x86/insn-eval: Move assign_register() out of KVM as insn_assign_reg()

KVM's instruction emulator has a small helper, assign_register(), that
writes a value into a register following the x86 rules for writes to
general-purpose registers: an 8- or 16-bit write leaves the rest of the
register untouched, a 32-bit write zero-extends the result to 64 bits,
and a 64-bit write replaces the whole register.

The TDX guest #VE handler needs the same logic for port I/O emulation
to get 32-bit zero-extension right.  Rather than add a third copy of
the same switch, move the helper verbatim to <asm/insn-eval.h>, rename
it to insn_assign_reg(), and route KVM's callers through it.

Add <asm/insn.h> to the header's includes so it builds standalone in
callers that have not pulled it in transitively.

No functional change.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta) <kas@kernel.org>
Signed-off-by: Dave Hansen <dave.hansen@linux.intel.com>
Acked-by: Sean Christopherson <seanjc@google.com>

index 4733e90..ae05647 100644
--- a/arch/x86/include/asm/insn-eval.h
+++ b/arch/x86/include/asm/insn-eval.h
@@ -9,6 +9,7 @@
 #include <linux/compiler.h>
 #include <linux/bug.h>
 #include <linux/err.h>
+#include <asm/insn.h>
 #include <asm/ptrace.h>
 
 #define INSN_CODE_SEG_ADDR_SZ(params) ((params >> 4) & 0xf)
@@ -46,4 +47,39 @@ enum insn_mmio_type insn_decode_mmio(struct insn *insn, int *bytes);
 
 bool insn_is_nop(struct insn *insn);
 
+/*
+ * Write @val into *@reg following the x86 rules for writes to
+ * general-purpose registers (Intel SDM Vol. 1, "General-Purpose
+ * Registers in 64-Bit Mode"): an 8- or 16-bit write leaves the rest of
+ * the register untouched, a 32-bit write zero-extends the result into
+ * the upper 32 bits, and a 64-bit write replaces the whole register.
+ *
+ * @bytes is the width of the write, not a property of the instruction:
+ * an instruction that, say, sign-extends a 32-bit immediate into a
+ * 64-bit register does a 64-bit write here.
+ *
+ * @reg need not be 8-byte aligned: KVM's instruction emulator offsets
+ * the pointer by one byte to address the high-byte registers (AH, CH,
+ * DH, BH).  Use narrow stores for the sub-word cases so the access
+ * width matches @bytes and the adjacent bytes are left alone.
+ */
+static inline void insn_assign_reg(unsigned long *reg, u64 val, int bytes)
+{
+	switch (bytes) {
+	case 1:
+		*(u8 *)reg = (u8)val;
+		break;
+	case 2:
+		*(u16 *)reg = (u16)val;
+		break;
+	case 4:
+		/* A 32-bit write zero-extends into the upper 32 bits. */
+		*reg = (u32)val;
+		break;
+	case 8:
+		*reg = val;
+		break;
+	}
+}
+
 #endif /* _ASM_X86_INSN_EVAL_H */

The following commit has been merged into the x86/tdx branch of tip:

Commit-ID:     0f63e656b1c679d32ac595de29d10c03efca6a25
Gitweb:        https://git.kernel.org/tip/0f63e656b1c679d32ac595de29d10c03efca6a25
Author:        Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta) <kas@kernel.org>

Committer:     Dave Hansen <dave.hansen@linux.intel.com>
CommitterDate: Mon, 13 Jul 2026 14:45:07 -07:00

x86/tdx: Fix off-by-one in port I/O handling

handle_in() and handle_out() in arch/x86/coco/tdx/tdx.c use:

    u64 mask = GENMASK(BITS_PER_BYTE * size, 0);

GENMASK(h, l) includes bit h. For size=1 (INB), this produces
GENMASK(8, 0) = 0x1FF (9 bits) instead of GENMASK(7, 0) = 0xFF (8
bits). The mask is one bit too wide for all I/O sizes.

Fix the mask calculation.

Fixes: 03149948832a ("x86/tdx: Port I/O: Add runtime hypercalls")

Last comment 2026-07-16 Re: [PATCH v2 1/5] mm/memory-failure: keep the folio, not the poisoned subpage, locked across split Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a bug in the memory failure handling of huge pages by ensuring that the folio, not just its poisoned subpage, remains locked across split operations. The issue arises when the mapping is unlocked after the split, allowing concurrent final iput() to evict and RCU-free the inode before i_mmap_unlock_read() touches the i_mmap_rwsem. To fix this, the patch moves the unlock of i_mmap_rwsem to right after remap_page(), before the loop that unlocks and frees the after-split folios, and clears @mapping to prevent it from being unlocked again.

Re: [PATCH mm-hotfixes v2 3/4] mm/ptdump: always stabilise against page table freeing using init_mm Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Re: [PATCH mm-hotfixes v2 1/4] mm/vmalloc: acquire init_mm lock on huge vmap to avoid ptdump UAF Neutral LLM
Last comment 2026-07-13 Re: [PATCH mm-hotfixes v2 0/4] mm: fix UAF caused by race between ptdump and vmap pgtable freeing Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series addresses an out-of-bounds memory access (UAF) caused by a race between the ptdump and vmap pgtable freeing mechanisms in the Linux kernel. The issue arises from inverted locking, where the walker takes the write lock and mutators take read locks, which is subtle and prone to deadlocks. To fix this, the patch proposes deferring the free of page tables using RCU (Read-Copy Update) and making ptdump a lockless walker. This approach would eliminate the need for inverted locking and prevent potential deadlocks, while also improving performance by reducing the load on the init_mm mmap write lock.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Lorenzo (ARM) ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Lorenzo expressed concerns about backporting a fix that involves RCU-deferring page table freeing, citing potential subtleties and maintenance issues. He also noted that the ptdump walker on x86 needs to take the mmap write lock to exclude munmap operations, making it incompatible with an RCU-free approach. Reviewer noted that the patch's locking scheme is inverted from normal expectations, which led to an arm64 deadlock and ifdeffery, and suggested using RCU-deferred freeing for ptdump like GUP-fast does Reviewer noted that the locking scheme in the patch is inverted from what's normally expected, which led to an arm64 deadlock and ifdeffery, and suggested using RCU-deferred free and making ptdump a lockless walker Reviewer suggested using call_rcu() instead of synchronize_rcu(), citing it as a 'bug hammer', and noted that CPA doesn't mark page tables as kernel Reviewer noted that the current locking scheme in ptdump is inverted from normal expectations, which led to an arm64 deadlock and ifdeffery, and suggested revisiting the approach by using RCU-deferred free and making ptdump a lockless walker. The reviewer is concerned that ptdump's walk may stall due to RCU, especially considering vmap can span PUDs and P4Ds, and also worries about the validity of page table entries when read by the walker. They suggest making changes across the kernel for all kernel page table users.
Show original comment 
Overall I like the idea of eventually moving this to _all_ being
RCU-free-able :)

BUT... I don't like it as a fix for this bug that has to be backported.

I think there's a lot of subtleties to worry about and I don't want to
worry about having to maintain tht as a backport.

But also, we have an unfortunate situation with ptdump where it also walks
userland ranges on x86 (and ranges for efi_mm on both x86 and arm64).

And for userland ranges you have to have the mmap write lock to exclude a
downgraded mmap read lock munmap() operation (which gives rise to the weird
inversion you mention).

So the walker still has to take the write lock in this case.

I spoke a bit about it overall at [0].

We already have this mmap lock convention as a requirement for kernel
ranges, and it was being violated by CPA and vmalloc.

So I'd prefer we keep this as the proximate fix to solve the bug here, and
then revisit this later (alongside moving to RCU page table freeing
_overall_, though one doesn't have to block the other).

I believe Suren is looking at this (and I'm happy to look if Suren is tied
up with other things also!)

---

Yeah :) this is a direct result of the munmap() thing mentioned above
(because ptdump can read non-kernel ranges).

Because you _have_ to take the mmap write lock there, so you _may as well_
exclude with a read lock and then get to do an mmap read trylock as an
added bonus on vmap huge page promotion.

---

Well everybody who does things with shared resources should be aware of
locking requirements.

---

Well I did want to say btw that CPA doesn't actually mark the page tables
as kernel, was going to chase up with something on that when I got a chance
:)

synchronize_rcu() is a very bug hammer, you can just use call_rcu() (and
the ptdesc already has an rcu_head I think).

---

Yeah it all sucks, but you're really making my point that this is better to

---

Yes - as above obviously I'd definitely prefer to defer this to a
follow-up.

The main concern for ptdump is properly slicing its walk to avoid
RCU stalls.

Keeping in mind that vmap can (in theory) span PUDs and even P4Ds it
becomes a bit tricky.

But also I worry about whether the entries in the page table will actually
be valid at the point the walker reads them.

For vmap/CPA pretty much yes they are, but if something was to actually
unmap them in future then that might no longer be the case. RCU will only
guarantee that the page tables stick around, not that they contain anything
valid.

In userland a lot of this is solved already by the leaf page table entry's
PTL.

Kernel page tables are a bit of a weird case, because mostly we solve
things through exclusive owners, ptdump being the weirdo outlier.

Anyway TL;DR is that - I think we need to be careful and think this through
and make changes across the board for all kernel page table users and
update the page walkers.

I'm happy to spin up an RFC for this when I get a moment :)

But in general I'd like us to solve the problem as a whole across the
kernel for both userland and kernel page table allocations, ideally!

Kiryl Shutsemau (author) ↳ replying to Lorenzo (ARM) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed Lorenzo's concern about the inverted locking scheme in ptdump, explaining that walking user page tables under RCU can make the mmap lock unnecessary and proposing to remove it for all ranges, including kernel ones. Author agrees to restructure the patch in a follow-up series to address concerns about inverted locking and potential deadlocks, and promises to properly review the current patchset. Author acknowledged that CPA allocates split tables directly and thus skips IOMMU SVA KVA invalidation, which is an independent issue from the original patch. Author acknowledged a potential issue with bounding read-side sections during ptdump walks, agreeing it's the part that needs the most care and implying further work is needed. The author addressed Lorenzo's concern about stale page table entries being accessed by the walker, explaining that since frees are RCU-deferred, the walker can only reach a table via an entry it read within its RCU section and freeing requires unlinking that entry first. The author acknowledged that this approach is sufficient for ptdump but agreed that more care is needed for general cases.
Show original comment 
I think the userland side is fixable too.

My first thought was to walk only VMA-backed ranges, which would make
the mmap read lock sufficient: munmap() detaches VMAs under the write
lock before downgrading, and free_pgtables() only frees tables
exclusively covering the detached range.

But that is an unnecessary limitation on MMU_GATHER_RCU_TABLE_FREE
architectures -- which is every architecture with generic ptdump
support. There, user page table freeing is already RCU-deferred; it is
what GUP-fast relies on to walk user page tables blindly with no mmap
lock. khugepaged retracting PTE tables under the pmd lock is likewise
covered by pte_free_defer().

So the downgraded munmap() teardown you mention is already safe to race
with -- for a walker inside an RCU read-side section. The write lock is
only needed today because ptdump walks outside of one, so the deferred
freeing does nothing for it.

That would make the end state: the whole of ptdump -- userland, efi_mm
and kernel ranges -- walks under RCU with no mmap lock taken at all,
and the kernel-side freeing conversion we discussed is the only
missing piece.

---

No argument, let's do it as a follow-up.

I will give the current patchset a proper review.

---

You are right. CPA allocates the split tables with bare
pagetable_alloc(), so ptdesc_test_kernel() is false and collapse frees
them via __pagetable_free() directly. Note this also means they skip
the IOMMU SVA KVA invalidation that ASYNC_KERNEL_PGTABLE_FREE is there
for -- that looks like a bug today, independent of ptdump.

---

Since the walker only reads, I think it is enough to re-descend from
the pgd with fresh READ_ONCE() at each level after dropping RCU --
whatever level got promoted in the meantime, we see either the new leaf
or the old table. But agreed this is the part that needs the most care.

---

For a walker that only reads, I don't think staleness is a problem as
long as frees are RCU-deferred: the walker can only reach a table via
an entry it read within its RCU section, and freeing requires unlinking
that entry first, so the grace period covers any walker that saw the
old pointer. Stale leaf contents are harmless for a dumper. It does
become a problem for anything that dereferences through leaf entries --
agreed that the general case needs more care than ptdump does.

Re: [PATCH v5 2/2] selftests/mm: add PAGEMAP_SCAN test for THP PMD holes Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a test to the selftests/mm suite to ensure that PAGEMAP_SCAN correctly reports written state for PMD holes. The test creates a MAP_PRIVATE|MAP_ANON THP, uffd-wp's it, and then drops it with MADV_DONTNEED, leaving a pmd_none hole without a page table. The patch also includes a helper function to factor out the populate/drop/scan sequence from an existing test.

Last comment 2026-07-13 Re: [RFC PATCH 00/10] mm/swap: ghost swapfile with backend switching via Redirect entries Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series proposes a new mechanism to implement ghost swapfiles with backend switching via Redirect entries, addressing issues related to writeback and overcommitting in large-scale systems. The current implementation has bugs that break writeback completely, leading to swapoff leaks and reclaimed physical slots being held by ghost zswap swapfile slots. The proposed solution aims to simplify the logic and improve efficiency while handling redirection temporarily.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Chris Li ↳ replying to Nhat Pham Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Chris Li agrees that ghost swapfile writeback feature seems premature and suggests dropping it from this series to focus on generic swapfile growing, which can be more efficient without an additional indirection layer.
Show original comment 
Hi Nhat,

Please read my above email. I agree with you that the ghost swapfile
writeback feature seems a bit premature. I suggested dropping the
ghost swapfile writeback from this series and focusing on generic
swapfile growing, because the swap device can actually grow outside of
the virtual context as well.

Growing a swap device does not mean it has to have another xarray or
indirection sitting in front of it. That is what I want to see
happening. It is actually more efficient without it.

As to your VS series, I have already made my comments on earlier
series and I am not going to repeat myself too much. I suggest taking
a more incremental approach rather than trying to solve all the
problems at once. Another thing is that, is there any reason the VS
can't be done on a per swap file using the swap ops approach? Assume
you can modify the swap ops to fit your need. That way you don't force
zram to take your VS layer as an additional penalty.

Given the choice between two approaches, if everything else is equal,
one forces an extra layer while the other does not. I would pick the
later one any time. Again if one extra layer is compile time, the
other one extra layer is run time. If everything else is equal, I
would prefer the runtime layer.

Thanks

Chris

Signals: requested changes, suggested alternative approach
Last comment 2026-07-15 Re: [RFC PATCH 00/10] mm/swap: ghost swapfile with backend switching via Redirect entries Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series proposes a new design for ghost swapfiles with backend switching via Redirect entries, addressing the issue of writeback support and redesigning around it. The goal is to create a single coherent design that solves the larger problem. The approach involves solving one piece of the puzzle at a time, starting with an optimization patch series and then incorporating features such as dynamic address space allocation and xarray-based backend storage. The design aims to be cleaner and more efficient than existing solutions, while allowing for flexibility in terms of metadata management and swap cache operations.

I think I this is a bit confusing on my part. Let me clarify - I did
not mean having vswap backed by ghost zswap swapfile backend here.
What I meant here was, "in order to support the use case similar to
ghost zswap swapfile", i.e zswap-without-writeback.

swap metadata follows Kairui's swap table design (and any advances he
made in optimizing the overhead will be applied immediately, since I
pretty much did not change the swap cache and swap count operations at
all).
